Why You Must Read This: Understanding "gating + additive memory channel" is the conceptual origin of all modern "selective memory" mechanisms—Mamba's selective state, Transformer's attention gating. This is the root from which they grew.

P05BatchNorm: Making Deep Network Training Simple

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 3, §3.2.5 Normalization

Batch Normalization: Accelerating Deep Network Training by Reducing Internal Covariate Shift
AuthorsSergey Ioffe, Christian Szegedy
AffiliationGoogle
PublishedICML 2015
Citations60,000+
LinkarXiv:1502.03167

The Problem: Why Deep Network Training Is Exquisitely Sensitive to Learning Rate

Ioffe observed that the input distribution of each layer shifts dramatically as the parameters of earlier layers are updated. This is called "internal covariate shift." The consequence is that the next layer must constantly "chase" the shifting distribution, making training unstable; moreover, different layers have optimal learning rates differing by orders of magnitude, making a single global learning rate a painful choice.

Core Method

For each feature channel within a mini-batch, apply standardization:

\hat{x}_i = \frac{x_i - \mu_B}{\sqrt{\sigma_B^2 + \epsilon}}, \quad y_i = \gamma \hat{x}_i + \beta

\mu_B, \sigma_B^2 are the batch statistics; \gamma, \beta are learnable scale and shift parameters—allowing the network to "restore" the pre-normalization distribution, but without constraining the upstream layers' parameters.

Training vs. Inference: during training, batch statistics are used; during inference, "running mean/variance" (maintained via exponential averaging during training) is used. This distinction is a common source of subtle BN bugs—it is a common reason why a model performs well on the training set but poorly at inference.

The Debate Over "Why BN Works"

The original paper attributed the effect to "reducing internal covariate shift." But a 2018 paper from MIT by Santurkar et al., "How Does Batch Normalization Help Optimization?", experimentally demonstrated that covariate shift has little to do with BN's effectiveness. The true reason is more likely that BN smooths the loss landscape, preventing gradient explosion even with large learning rates.

This is a classic pattern in deep learning research: a method is shown to work, but the reason it works turns out to be different from what the authors claimed.

Impact

BN became standard equipment for CNNs after 2015. It made training 100+ layer networks possible and, together with ResNet, supported the deep era of computer vision.

However, BN does not work well for RNNs or Transformers—batch-dimension statistics carry little meaning for sequential tasks. Transformers use LayerNorm throughout, which later evolved into RMSNorm. This is why, in main text §4.2.4, you see "Pre-Norm + RMSNorm" as the standard for LLMs. BN's story is largely confined to the CV domain.

Why You Must Read This: Understanding that "normalization is not data preprocessing—it is a structural component of deep networks"—this shift in perspective is BatchNorm's true contribution. Reading this paper will give you a foundational understanding of why LayerNorm, RMSNorm, and GroupNorm exist in the main text.

P06Adam: The Default Optimizer for Deep Learning

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 1, §1.7.4 · Chapter 3, §3.5.1

Adam: A Method for Stochastic Optimization
AuthorsDiederik P. Kingma, Jimmy Ba
AffiliationUniversity of Amsterdam, University of Toronto
PublishedICLR 2015
Citations200,000+
LinkarXiv:1412.6980

The Problem: SGD's Learning Rate Is Hard to Tune

Pure SGD requires careful learning rate scheduling—different learning rates for different parameters and different training phases. AdaGrad (Duchi 2011) adaptively adjusts the learning rate per parameter based on the accumulated sum of squared gradients—parameters seen frequently get a smaller lr, new parameters get a larger lr. Problem: the accumulation only grows, so in the later stages of training all learning rates converge to zero and training stops. RMSProp (Hinton 2012 course notes) replaces accumulation with an exponential moving average, solving the "permanent decay" problem.

Adam = RMSProp + momentum, plus a bias correction.

Core Algorithm

Two states are maintained: a first-moment estimate (momentum) \mathbf{m}_t and a second-moment estimate (exponential moving average of squared gradients) \mathbf{v}_t.

\mathbf{m}_t = \beta_1 \mathbf{m}_{t-1} + (1 - \beta_1) \mathbf{g}_t
\mathbf{v}_t = \beta_2 \mathbf{v}_{t-1} + (1 - \beta_2) \mathbf{g}_t^2

Bias correction—initially \mathbf{m}_0 = \mathbf{v}_0 = 0, so the exponential average is biased low; dividing by (1 - \beta^t) compensates:

\hat{\mathbf{m}}_t = \frac{\mathbf{m}_t}{1 - \beta_1^t}, \quad \hat{\mathbf{v}}_t = \frac{\mathbf{v}_t}{1 - \beta_2^t}

Final parameter update:

\boldsymbol{\theta}_{t+1} = \boldsymbol{\theta}_t - \frac{\alpha}{\sqrt{\hat{\mathbf{v}}_t} + \epsilon} \hat{\mathbf{m}}_t

Typical hyperparameters: \beta_1 = 0.9, \beta_2 = 0.999, \epsilon = 10^{-8}, \alpha = 10^{-3}. These numbers are the defaults in virtually every PyTorch/TensorFlow codebase.

AdamW

Loshchilov (2017) pointed out that "weight decay" regularization in Adam is incorrectly coupled with the learning rate. AdamW decouples them and is now the default choice for LLM training. The "AdamW" in LLaMA's training recipe in main text §4.7.4 refers to this.

Impact and Controversy

Adam is the most widely used optimizer in deep learning—present by default like air. But it has not been without controversy:

  • - Convergence: Reddi et al. (2018), "On the Convergence of Adam," proved that the original Adam can fail to converge on certain simple convex problems. AMSGrad was proposed as a fix (but is rarely used in practice)
  • - Generalization: On CV tasks, SGD+Momentum often generalizes better. But for Transformer training, Adam/AdamW has no viable alternative
  • - Memory: Adam requires storing \mathbf{m} and \mathbf{v}—twice the parameter footprint. In the era of large models, this is a GPU memory killer. ZeRO (main text §4.7.2) was created specifically to shard this state
Why You Must Read This: Every LLM training run uses AdamW under the hood. This is the power of defaults—but knowing the math behind the defaults lets you diagnose training instabilities when they arise.

P07XGBoost: The De Facto Standard for Tabular Data

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 3, §3.1.2 Tree Models

XGBoost: A Scalable Tree Boosting System
AuthorsTianqi Chen, Carlos Guestrin
AffiliationUniversity of Washington
PublishedKDD 2016
Citations30,000+
LinkarXiv:1603.02754

The Problem: Two Bottlenecks of Traditional GBDT

Traditional GBDT (Friedman 2001) fits each tree to the residuals from the previous step (equivalent to the negative gradient for squared loss). Two problems:

  1. Only first-order gradients: limited optimization direction information, slow convergence
  2. Engineering-level slowness: single-machine training on large datasets takes hours or more; distributed implementations were absent

Core Method: Second-Order Taylor Expansion + Regularization + Parallelism

Tianqi Chen applied a second-order Taylor expansion of the objective function at the current model:

\mathcal{L}^{(t)} \approx \sum_i \left[ g_i f_t(\mathbf{x}_i) + \frac{1}{2} h_i f_t(\mathbf{x}_i)^2 \right] + \Omega(f_t)

where g_i = \partial L / \partial F (first-order), h_i = \partial^2 L / \partial F^2 (second-order), and \Omega(f) = \gamma T + \frac{1}{2} \lambda \sum_j w_j^2 is the regularization term (T is the number of leaves, w_j are leaf weights).

For a tree with a fixed structure, the optimal weight of each leaf has a closed-form solution:

w_j^* = -\frac{\sum_{i \in I_j} g_i}{\sum_{i \in I_j} h_i + \lambda}

The split gain also has a closed form:

\text{Gain} = \frac{1}{2}\left[\frac{G_L^2}{H_L + \lambda} + \frac{G_R^2}{H_R + \lambda} - \frac{(G_L+G_R)^2}{H_L+H_R+\lambda}\right] - \gamma

With this formula, a greedy search picks the split with the highest gain until gain < 0 (or another stopping criterion). A detailed derivation is in main text §3.1.2.

Engineering Innovations

  • Approximate split algorithm: for large datasets, rather than enumerating all split points, discretize by quantiles (LightGBM later pushed this to the extreme with its histogram algorithm)
  • Sparsity-aware: missing values are automatically routed to a "default direction," requiring no manual imputation
  • Column-block storage: features are stored in compressed column format, sorted once and reused across rounds
  • Parallelism: feature-level parallelism (each feature independently searches for its split point)
  • Cache-aware access: prefetching data into CPU cache to improve hit rates

Python Usage

import xgboost as xgb
from sklearn.datasets import load_breast_cancer
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split

X, y = load_breast_cancer(return_X_y=True)
Xt, Xv, yt, yv = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=0.2)

model = xgb.XGBClassifier(
    n_estimators=500, max_depth=6, learning_rate=0.05,
    reg_lambda=1.0, reg_alpha=0.1,
    early_stopping_rounds=50, eval_metric='logloss',
)
model.fit(Xt, yt, eval_set=[(Xv, yv)])
print("Accuracy:", model.score(Xv, yv))

Impact

From 2015 to 2020, 90%+ of winning Kaggle tabular competition solutions included XGBoost, LightGBM, or CatBoost. These three tools are all essentially variants of the XGBoost approach:

  • LightGBM (Microsoft 2017): histogram-based acceleration, leaf-wise growth instead of level-wise
  • CatBoost (Yandex 2018): native categorical feature handling, ordered boosting

In industry, XGBoost remains the first choice for tabular data. The reasons: fast training and inference (millions of samples in minutes), no GPU required, no feature normalization needed, and naturally interpretable feature importance. Deep learning has long had no advantage on tabular data—TabNet and TabTransformer never became mainstream.

Why You Must Read This: Even in the LLM era, this classic paper remains important—because in financial risk management, healthcare, recommendation systems, and network traffic classification, XGBoost comprehensively outperforms deep learning on latency and interpretability. Knowing when to use it and when not to is a basic judgment call for any AI engineer.
PART TWO

Ch. 4 · Transformer & Pretraining

The foundational pillars of the LLM era — Attention, BERT/GPT, Scaling Laws, and RoPE.

P08GAN: The Birth of Adversarial Training

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 3, Section 3.3.4 — Generative Models

Generative Adversarial Nets
AuthorsIan J. Goodfellow, Jean Pouget-Abadie, Mehdi Mirza, Bing Xu, David Warde-Farley, Sherjil Ozair, Aaron Courville, Yoshua Bengio
AffiliationUniversite de Montreal
PublishedNeurIPS 2014
Citations70,000+
LinkarXiv:1406.2661

The Problem: Why Generative Models Were So Hard

Before 2014, the dominant generative models were: Restricted Boltzmann Machines (RBMs), Variational Autoencoders (VAEs, introduced the same year), and autoregressive models (PixelRNN would not arrive for another two years). Their shared limitation: either they required explicitly defining a likelihood function and performing complex inference, or they produced blurry outputs. Goodfellow, while discussing the problem with a friend at a bar, conceived a way to circumvent the likelihood function entirely — have two networks engage in adversarial competition.

Core Method: Two-Player Zero-Sum Game

Two networks are trained simultaneously:

  • Generator G : generates fake samples G(z) from noise z \sim p_z(z), with the goal of "fooling the discriminator"
  • Discriminator D: judges whether an input is a real or generated sample, with the goal of "not being fooled"

Objective Function

\min_G \max_D V(D, G) = \mathbb{E}_{x \sim p_{\text{data}}}[\log D(x)] + \mathbb{E}_{z \sim p_z}[\log(1 - D(G(z)))]

The game-theoretic meaning of this minimax: D wants to maximize its discriminative ability (pulling V up), while G wants to minimize D's discriminative ability (pushing V down).

Theoretical Results

Goodfellow proves two key theorems:

  1. Optimal discriminator: given G, the optimal D^*(x) = \frac{p_{\text{data}}(x)}{p_{\text{data}}(x) + p_g(x)}
  2. Global optimum: when p_g = p_{\text{data}}, D^* = 1/2 and V reaches its global minimum of -\log 4. That is, at equilibrium the generated distribution perfectly matches the data distribution.

Substituting D^* into V reveals that V is equivalent to a transformation of the Jensen-Shannon divergence between the data distribution and the generated distribution:

V(D^*, G) = -\log 4 + 2 \cdot \text{JSD}(p_{\text{data}} \| p_g)

Training Loop

flowchart LR Z["Noise z ~ N(0,I)"] --> G["Generator G"] G --> FakeImg["Fake samples G(z)"] RealImg["Real samples x"] --> D["Discriminator D"] FakeImg --> D D --> Loss["D: max log D(x) + log(1-D(G(z))) G: min log(1-D(G(z)))"] 
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Generator(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, z_dim=100, img_dim=784):
        super().__init__()
        self.net = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(z_dim, 256), nn.LeakyReLU(0.2),
            nn.Linear(256, 512), nn.LeakyReLU(0.2),
            nn.Linear(512, img_dim), nn.Tanh(),
        )

    def forward(self, z):
        return self.net(z)

class Discriminator(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, img_dim=784):
        super().__init__()
        self.net = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(img_dim, 512), nn.LeakyReLU(0.2),
            nn.Linear(512, 256), nn.LeakyReLU(0.2),
            nn.Linear(256, 1), nn.Sigmoid(),
        )

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.net(x)

# Training loop
z_dim = 100
G = Generator(z_dim)
D = Discriminator()
opt_G = torch.optim.Adam(G.parameters(), lr=2e-4, betas=(0.5, 0.999))
opt_D = torch.optim.Adam(D.parameters(), lr=2e-4, betas=(0.5, 0.999))
bce = nn.BCELoss()

for epoch in range(num_epochs):
    for real_batch, _ in dataloader:
        batch_size = real_batch.size(0)
        real_batch = real_batch.view(batch_size, -1)

        # --- Train discriminator ---
        z = torch.randn(batch_size, z_dim)
        fake = G(z).detach()
        loss_D = bce(D(real_batch), torch.ones(batch_size, 1)) + \
                 bce(D(fake), torch.zeros(batch_size, 1))
        opt_D.zero_grad(); loss_D.backward(); opt_D.step()

        # --- Train generator ---
        z = torch.randn(batch_size, z_dim)
        fake = G(z)
        # Trick: use -log D(G(z)) instead of log(1 - D(G(z))) for stronger gradients
        loss_G = bce(D(fake), torch.ones(batch_size, 1))
        opt_G.zero_grad(); loss_G.backward(); opt_G.step()

Training Instability: GAN's "Original Sin"

GAN's theory is elegant, but training in practice is extremely difficult:

  • Mode collapse: G learns to produce only a few types of samples that are sufficient to fool D, sacrificing diversity
  • Training oscillation: G and D must stay balanced in capability — if one becomes too strong, the other receives no useful learning signal
  • Vanishing gradients: when D is too accurate, \log(1 - D(G(z))) \to 0 and G receives no gradient

A large body of subsequent work addressed these issues: WGAN (replacing JSD with Wasserstein distance), SNGAN (spectral normalization), Progressive GAN (progressively increasing resolution), StyleGAN (disentangling style and content).

Impact and Decline

From 2014 to 2021, GANs were the undisputed leaders in image generation. StyleGAN2 (2019) produced synthetic faces that remain the benchmark for "fake faces." But in 2022, diffusion models (DDPM / Stable Diffusion) surpassed GANs comprehensively in both quality and diversity, and GAN's dominant position rapidly eroded. By 2025, GANs are primarily used for:

  1. Real-time applications (inference requires only one forward pass, roughly 100× faster than diffusion models)
  2. Specific tasks such as super-resolution and style transfer
  3. Accelerating diffusion models (e.g., consistency distillation uses adversarial training)

Critical Perspective

The "adversarial training" idea outlasts the GAN architecture itself. The reward model in RLHF that discriminates between "good" and "bad" responses is, in a sense, a discriminator; adversarial example attacks and defenses (adversarial robustness) draw directly from GAN's game-theoretic framework. Goodfellow himself later shifted toward adversarial security research.

Why You Must Read This: "Adversarial training" is one of the most important paradigms in deep learning. Even though GANs have ceded ground in image generation to diffusion models, their game-theoretic perspective has influenced RLHF, adversarial robustness, and data augmentation, among other areas. Understanding GANs is what lets you understand why RLHF requires a reward model.

P09VAE: Variational Inference Meets Deep Learning

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 3, Section 3.3.4 — Generative Models

Auto-Encoding Variational Bayes
AuthorsDiederik P. Kingma, Max Welling
AffiliationUniversity of Amsterdam
PublishedICLR 2014
Citations45,000+
LinkarXiv:1312.6114

The Problem: A Missing Probabilistic Framework for Deep Generative Models

GANs sidestep the likelihood function, but at the cost of training instability and an inability to compute probabilities. Traditional Bayesian methods support probabilistic inference but cannot handle high-dimensional data (images, text). Kingma and Welling's question: can we use neural networks for variational inference while preserving a probabilistic framework?

Core Method: The Variational Lower Bound (ELBO)

Generative Model Assumption

Assume data x is generated by latent variable z: first sample z \sim p_\theta(z) (prior, typically a standard Gaussian), then decode x \sim p_\theta(x|z) using a neural network. We want to maximize the marginal likelihood of the data:

\log p_\theta(x) = \log \int p_\theta(x|z) p_\theta(z) \, dz

This integral is intractable for high-dimensional z.

Variational Inference

Introduce an "encoder" q_\phi(z|x) to approximate the true posterior p_\theta(z|x). Applying Jensen's inequality yields the ELBO (Evidence Lower BOund):

\log p_\theta(x) \geq \underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{q_\phi(z|x)}[\log p_\theta(x|z)]}_{\text{reconstruction term}} - \underbrace{D_{\text{KL}}(q_\phi(z|x) \| p_\theta(z))}_{\text{regularization term}} = \text{ELBO}

The intuition behind each term:

  • Reconstruction term: how well x can be recovered from z (maximize)
  • KL divergence term: prevents the encoder's output distribution from straying too far from the prior (minimize; encourages a structured latent space)

The Reparameterization Trick

The key technical innovation: sampling from q_\phi(z|x) = \mathcal{N}(\mu_\phi(x), \sigma^2_\phi(x)) does not allow gradients to flow back through the sampling operation (which is non-differentiable). Kingma's solution: factor out the randomness:

z = \mu_\phi(x) + \sigma_\phi(x) \odot \epsilon, \quad \epsilon \sim \mathcal{N}(0, I)

Now z is a deterministic function of \phi (with external noise \epsilon), and gradients can backpropagate normally.

flowchart LR X["Input x"] --> Enc["Encoder q_phi"] Enc --> Mu["mu"] Enc --> Sigma["sigma"] Eps["epsilon ~ N(0,I)"] --> RP["z = mu + sigma * epsilon"] Mu --> RP Sigma --> RP RP --> Dec["Decoder p_theta"] Dec --> Xhat["Reconstruction x'"]

PyTorch Implementation

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class VAE(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_dim=784, z_dim=20):
        super().__init__()
        # Encoder
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(input_dim, 400)
        self.fc_mu = nn.Linear(400, z_dim)
        self.fc_logvar = nn.Linear(400, z_dim)
        # Decoder
        self.fc3 = nn.Linear(z_dim, 400)
        self.fc4 = nn.Linear(400, input_dim)

    def encode(self, x):
        h = F.relu(self.fc1(x))
        return self.fc_mu(h), self.fc_logvar(h)

    def reparameterize(self, mu, logvar):
        std = torch.exp(0.5 * logvar)
        eps = torch.randn_like(std)       # external noise
        return mu + std * eps             # reparameterization

    def decode(self, z):
        h = F.relu(self.fc3(z))
        return torch.sigmoid(self.fc4(h))

    def forward(self, x):
        mu, logvar = self.encode(x.view(-1, 784))
        z = self.reparameterize(mu, logvar)
        return self.decode(z), mu, logvar

def vae_loss(recon_x, x, mu, logvar):
    # Reconstruction loss (binary cross-entropy)
    BCE = F.binary_cross_entropy(recon_x, x.view(-1, 784), reduction='sum')
    # KL divergence: -0.5 * sum(1 + log(sigma^2) - mu^2 - sigma^2)
    KLD = -0.5 * torch.sum(1 + logvar - mu.pow(2) - logvar.exp())
    return BCE + KLD

VAE's "Blurriness" Problem and Subsequent Developments

VAE's generation quality (especially for images) has always lagged behind GANs — outputs tend to be blurry. The cause: pixel-level reconstruction loss (MSE/BCE) encourages "averaging" over all plausible outputs. Subsequent improvements include:

  • VQ-VAE (van den Oord, 2017): discrete latent space replacing the continuous Gaussian, yielding sharper generations
  • VQ-VAE-2 (Razavi, 2019): hierarchical discrete codes, quality approaching BigGAN
  • Stable Diffusion: diffusion performed in VQ-VAE's latent space — the VAE handles compression, diffusion handles generation. This is the standard image generation pipeline of 2023–2025.

Impact

VAE's influence extends well beyond the application of image generation:

  1. Reparameterization trick: has become the standard method for any setting that requires backpropagating gradients through sampling operations (reinforcement learning, Gumbel-Softmax, etc.)
  2. Latent space learning: VAE defines the "encoder–latent space–decoder" paradigm, which directly shaped the Latent Diffusion architecture of Stable Diffusion
  3. Variational inference + deep learning: opened the door to probabilistic deep learning, spawning variational sequence models, variational graph neural networks, and more
Why You Must Read This: VAE is a prerequisite for understanding the Stable Diffusion architecture — the "Latent" in Latent Diffusion is exactly the VAE's latent space. The reparameterization trick is also widely used in the LLM domain (e.g., Gumbel-Softmax for discrete selection). Kingma is the author of both Adam and VAE; together, those two papers may constitute the highest-citation combination in all of deep learning.

P10DDPM: Diffusion Models, the Foundation of Modern Image Generation

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 3, Section 3.3.4 — Generative Models

Denoising Diffusion Probabilistic Models
AuthorsJonathan Ho, Ajay Jain, Pieter Abbeel
AffiliationUC Berkeley
PublishedNeurIPS 2020
Citations15,000+
LinkarXiv:2006.11239

The Problem: Where Is the Alternative to GANs?

In 2020, GANs (particularly StyleGAN2) held the highest image generation quality, but training instability and mode collapse continued to frustrate the community. The theoretical framework of diffusion models had been proposed as early as 2015 by Sohl-Dickstein, but generation quality remained far below GANs. Ho et al.'s contribution was the first time a diffusion model matched GAN quality — and with dramatically simpler training.

Core Method: Forward Noising + Reverse Denoising

Forward Process (Diffusion)

Starting from a real image x_0, Gaussian noise is added incrementally over T steps until the result is pure noise:

q(x_t | x_{t-1}) = \mathcal{N}(x_t; \sqrt{1 - \beta_t} \, x_{t-1}, \beta_t I)

Here \beta_t is a predefined noise schedule that increases from small to large (e.g., \beta_1 = 10^{-4} to \beta_T = 0.02). A key property: one can jump directly from x_0 to any x_t without sequential computation:

q(x_t | x_0) = \mathcal{N}(x_t; \sqrt{\bar{\alpha}_t} \, x_0, (1 - \bar{\alpha}_t) I)

where \alpha_t = 1 - \beta_t and \bar{\alpha}_t = \prod_{s=1}^{t} \alpha_s. For sufficiently large T, x_T \approx \mathcal{N}(0, I).

Reverse Process (Denoising)

If we can learn to reverse each step — predicting x_{t-1} from x_t — we can generate images starting from pure noise. The reverse process is also Gaussian:

p_\theta(x_{t-1} | x_t) = \mathcal{N}(x_{t-1}; \mu_\theta(x_t, t), \sigma_t^2 I)

Ho et al.'s key simplification: instead of directly predicting \mu, the network predicts the noise \epsilon . The network \epsilon_\theta(x_t, t) takes a noisy image and a timestep as input and outputs the predicted noise.

flowchart LR subgraph Forward["Forward Process (fixed)"] X0["x_0 (original)"] -->|"add noise"| X1["x_1"] X1 -->|"add noise"| X2["x_2"] X2 -->|"..."| XT["x_T (pure noise)"] end subgraph Reverse["Reverse Process (learned)"] XT2["x_T ~ N(0,I)"] -->|"denoise"| XTm1["x_{T-1}"] XTm1 -->|"denoise"| XTm2["x_{T-2}"] XTm2 -->|"..."| X02["x_0 (generated)"] end

Training Objective: Radical Simplification

DDPM's training loss is the simplest of any deep generative model:

L_{\text{simple}} = \mathbb{E}_{t, x_0, \epsilon}\left[\|\epsilon - \epsilon_\theta(\sqrt{\bar{\alpha}_t} x_0 + \sqrt{1-\bar{\alpha}_t} \epsilon, \, t)\|^2\right]

In plain terms: randomly sample a timestep t, randomly sample noise \epsilon, add the noise to x_0 to obtain x_t, have the network predict the added noise, and train with MSE. That is all.

Training and Sampling Code

import torch
import torch.nn as nn

# Noise schedule
def linear_schedule(T, beta_start=1e-4, beta_end=0.02):
    return torch.linspace(beta_start, beta_end, T)

T = 1000
betas = linear_schedule(T)
alphas = 1.0 - betas
alpha_bars = torch.cumprod(alphas, dim=0)

# Forward noising: jump directly from x_0 to x_t
def q_sample(x_0, t, noise=None):
    if noise is None:
        noise = torch.randn_like(x_0)
    sqrt_alpha_bar = alpha_bars[t].sqrt().view(-1, 1, 1, 1)
    sqrt_one_minus = (1 - alpha_bars[t]).sqrt().view(-1, 1, 1, 1)
    return sqrt_alpha_bar * x_0 + sqrt_one_minus * noise, noise

# Training loop
model = UNet(...)  # U-Net that predicts noise
optimizer = torch.optim.Adam(model.parameters(), lr=2e-4)

for batch in dataloader:
    x_0 = batch['image']
    t = torch.randint(0, T, (x_0.size(0),))       # random timestep
    x_t, noise = q_sample(x_0, t)                   # add noise
    pred_noise = model(x_t, t)                       # predict noise
    loss = nn.functional.mse_loss(pred_noise, noise) # just MSE
    optimizer.zero_grad(); loss.backward(); optimizer.step()

# Sampling: iteratively denoise from pure noise
@torch.no_grad()
def sample(model, shape):
    x = torch.randn(shape)  # x_T ~ N(0, I)
    for t in reversed(range(T)):
        t_batch = torch.full((shape[0],), t, dtype=torch.long)
        pred_noise = model(x, t_batch)
        # Compute mean of x_{t-1}
        alpha = alphas[t]; alpha_bar = alpha_bars[t]
        x = (1 / alpha.sqrt()) * (x - (1 - alpha) / (1 - alpha_bar).sqrt() * pred_noise)
        if t > 0:
            x = x + betas[t].sqrt() * torch.randn_like(x)  # reintroduce stochasticity
    return x

Why Diffusion Models Surpassed GANs

DimensionGANDDPM
Training stabilityPoor (minimax game)Good (simple MSE)
Mode coverageProne to mode collapseNaturally covers all modes (iterative denoising)
Generation diversityLimitedVery high
Inference speedOne forward pass (fast)Requires T steps (slow)
Conditional generationRequires specialized architectureClassifier-free guidance suffices

Downstream Development Chain

  1. DDIM (Song, 2020): deterministic sampling — 50 steps is enough, no need for 1000
  2. Classifier-Free Guidance (Ho & Salimans, 2022): conditional generation without a separate classifier, dramatically improving quality
  3. Latent Diffusion / Stable Diffusion (Rombach, 2022): diffusion in VAE latent space; 64× compression makes the process tractable
  4. DALL-E 2/3, Midjourney, Imagen: text-to-image generation, all based on diffusion
  5. Sora (OpenAI, 2024): video generation using diffusion + Transformer (DiT)

Critical Perspective

The inference speed problem of diffusion models is not fully solved — compressing 1000 steps to 4–8 (consistency distillation) incurs quality degradation. Additionally, the theoretical framework of diffusion models limits their applicability to discrete data (text) — text generation remains the domain of autoregressive models (GPT). There are some 2024–2025 attempts at discrete diffusion models (e.g., MDLM), but no substantial breakthrough has emerged yet.

Why You Must Read This: Stable Diffusion, DALL-E, Midjourney, Sora — every stunning image and video generation result from 2022–2025 builds on DDPM's ideas. Once you understand the mathematics of forward noising and reverse denoising, you can grasp why text-to-image generation requires so many inference steps, and why Latent Diffusion moves the diffusion process into latent space.

P11Word2Vec: The Starting Point of Word Embeddings

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 3, Section 3.5.2 — Embeddings

Efficient Estimation of Word Representations in Vector Space
AuthorsTomas Mikolov, Kai Chen, Greg Corrado, Jeffrey Dean
AffiliationGoogle
PublishedICLR 2013 Workshop
Citations40,000+
LinkarXiv:1301.3781

The Problem: A Mathematical Representation of Words

The first challenge in NLP: how do we turn words into numbers? Before 2013, the mainstream approach was one-hot vectors (a V-dimensional vector with a single 1, where V is the vocabulary size) or TF-IDF. The problems: one-hot vectors are extremely high-dimensional (vocabularies of tens to hundreds of thousands), and the distance between any two words is identical (zero semantic information).

Mikolov's goal: train dense, low-dimensional (100–300 dimensions) vectors such that semantically similar words are close together in the vector space.

Core Method: Two Architectures

CBOW (Continuous Bag of Words): predict the center word from the words in a context window. For example, given "the cat sat on the ___", use {the, cat, sat, on, the} to predict "mat."

Skip-gram: the reverse — predict the context from the center word. Given "sat", predict {cat, on}, etc. In practice, Skip-gram performs better (especially for rare words) and became the standard.

P(w_o | w_c) = \frac{\exp(\mathbf{v}_{w_o}' \cdot \mathbf{v}_{w_c})}{\sum_{w \in V} \exp(\mathbf{v}_w' \cdot \mathbf{v}_{w_c})}

Summing the denominator over the entire vocabulary is too slow. Two speedup strategies were introduced: Hierarchical Softmax (using a Huffman tree) and Negative Sampling (randomly sample a few "negatives" from the vocabulary and update only those). Negative Sampling is extremely simple and efficient and has become the de facto standard.

A Surprising Property: Vector Arithmetic

The most famous phenomenon discovered by Word2Vec:

\text{king} - \text{man} + \text{woman} \approx \text{queen}

Semantic relationships are encoded as directions in vector space. This finding directly shaped the design objectives of every subsequent embedding method.

Impact

Word2Vec ushered in the era of "everything can be embedded":

  • GloVe (Stanford, 2014): replaces the local window with a global co-occurrence matrix for a more elegant mathematical framework
  • FastText (Facebook, 2016): subword-level embeddings that handle out-of-vocabulary words
  • ELMo (Peters, 2018): contextual word vectors (the same word has different vectors in different sentences)
  • BERT/GPT: essentially upgrading "static embeddings" to "dynamic contextual embeddings"

By 2025, no one uses Word2Vec for NLP tasks — BERT/GPT contextual embeddings have replaced them entirely. Yet Word2Vec's ideas live on: Item2Vec in recommendation systems, Node2Vec in graph embeddings, Code2Vec in code embeddings — all are extensions of Word2Vec's paradigm of "mapping discrete objects into continuous space."

Why You Must Read This: Word2Vec is the best entry point for understanding the concept of "embedding." The token embeddings in BERT, the positional encodings in Transformers, the vector retrieval in RAG — all of these trace their intellectual origins to "representing discrete symbols with low-dimensional dense vectors." Mikolov's paper transformed this idea from an academic concept into an engineering standard.
PART SEVEN

Chapter 4 Related · LLM Supplements

Challengers to the Transformer, retrieval-augmented generation, and the reasoning framework for Agents.

Ch 4 Related · Transformer & Pretraining

P12Attention Is All You Need: The Birth of the Transformer

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 4, §4.2 (entire section)

Attention Is All You Need
AuthorsAshish Vaswani, Noam Shazeer, Niki Parmar, Jakob Uszkoreit, Llion Jones, Aidan N. Gomez, Łukasz Kaiser, Illia Polosukhin
AffiliationGoogle Brain, Google Research, University of Toronto
PublishedNeurIPS 2017
Citations140,000+ (second highest in CS history)
LinkarXiv:1706.03762

The Problem: The Fundamental Limitation of RNNs

In 2017, the mainstream approach to machine translation was seq2seq + attention (Bahdanau 2014, Luong 2015)—the encoder used an LSTM to encode the source sentence into a sequence of vectors, the decoder used an LSTM to autoregressively generate output, and attention was used to consult the encoder's output. The results were good, but LSTM's sequential nature across timesteps made training impossible to parallelize—the advantages of GPU acceleration could not be exploited, limiting model scale.

Vaswani et al.'s question: can we remove recurrence entirely and rely on attention alone?

Core Method: Scaled Dot-Product Attention

Each position in the sequence is represented as three vectors—Query, Key, and Value—and the output is computed by weighting V according to the similarity between Q and all Ks:

\text{Attention}(Q, K, V) = \text{softmax}\left(\frac{QK^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}}\right) V

Key detail: the \sqrt{d_k} scaling. The variance of the dot product of d-dimensional vectors is d; dividing by \sqrt{d} normalizes the variance and prevents the softmax from becoming oversaturated (main text §4.2.2 contains a detailed derivation).

Multi-Head Attention

Q, K, and V are projected into H subspaces; attention is computed independently in each subspace, and the results are concatenated and projected:

\text{MultiHead}(Q, K, V) = \text{Concat}(\text{head}_1, \ldots, \text{head}_H) W^O
\text{head}_i = \text{Attention}(Q W_i^Q, K W_i^K, V W_i^V)

Multiple heads let the model simultaneously learn multiple "attention patterns"—some heads focus on syntactic proximity, others on coreference, others on global topic.

Complete Transformer Block

flowchart TB X[Input x] --> N1[LayerNorm] N1 --> MHA[Multi-Head
Attention] MHA --> A1((+)) X --> A1 A1 --> N2[LayerNorm] N2 --> FFN[FFN
d_model to 4d to d_model] FFN --> A2((+)) A1 --> A2 A2 --> OUT[Output]

(The diagram shows the Pre-Norm version; the original paper used Post-Norm, but most modern implementations have switched to Pre-Norm. See main text §4.2.4.)

Positional Encoding

Attention itself is a "set operation"—permuting the input order leaves the output unchanged. To give the model a sense of position, positional encodings are added:

\text{PE}(\text{pos}, 2i) = \sin(\text{pos}/10000^{2i/d})
\text{PE}(\text{pos}, 2i+1) = \cos(\text{pos}/10000^{2i/d})

Position information is encoded using sinusoids of different frequencies. This was later superseded by RoPE (see P09).

Complete PyTorch Implementation

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import math

class MultiHeadAttention(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, d_model, n_heads):
        super().__init__()
        assert d_model % n_heads == 0
        self.H = n_heads
        self.d_head = d_model // n_heads
        self.Wq = nn.Linear(d_model, d_model, bias=False)
        self.Wk = nn.Linear(d_model, d_model, bias=False)
        self.Wv = nn.Linear(d_model, d_model, bias=False)
        self.Wo = nn.Linear(d_model, d_model, bias=False)

    def forward(self, x, mask=None):
        B, L, D = x.shape
        Q = self.Wq(x).view(B, L, self.H, self.d_head).transpose(1, 2)
        K = self.Wk(x).view(B, L, self.H, self.d_head).transpose(1, 2)
        V = self.Wv(x).view(B, L, self.H, self.d_head).transpose(1, 2)

        scores = Q @ K.transpose(-2, -1) / math.sqrt(self.d_head)  # [B, H, L, L]
        if mask is not None:
            scores = scores.masked_fill(mask, float('-inf'))
        attn = torch.softmax(scores, dim=-1)
        out = attn @ V                              # [B, H, L, d_head]
        out = out.transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, L, D)
        return self.Wo(out)


class FFN(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, d_model, d_ff=None):
        super().__init__()
        d_ff = d_ff or 4 * d_model
        self.fc1 = nn.Linear(d_model, d_ff)
        self.fc2 = nn.Linear(d_ff, d_model)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.fc2(torch.relu(self.fc1(x)))


class TransformerBlock(nn.Module):
    """Pre-Norm version — standard for modern LLMs"""
    def __init__(self, d_model, n_heads):
        super().__init__()
        self.ln1 = nn.LayerNorm(d_model)
        self.attn = MultiHeadAttention(d_model, n_heads)
        self.ln2 = nn.LayerNorm(d_model)
        self.ffn = FFN(d_model)

    def forward(self, x, mask=None):
        x = x + self.attn(self.ln1(x), mask)
        x = x + self.ffn(self.ln2(x))
        return x


class Transformer(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, vocab_size, d_model=512, n_heads=8, n_layers=6, max_len=5000):
        super().__init__()
        self.tok_emb = nn.Embedding(vocab_size, d_model)
        self.pos_emb = self._build_pos_enc(max_len, d_model)
        self.blocks = nn.ModuleList([
            TransformerBlock(d_model, n_heads) for _ in range(n_layers)
        ])
        self.ln_f = nn.LayerNorm(d_model)
        self.lm_head = nn.Linear(d_model, vocab_size, bias=False)

    def _build_pos_enc(self, max_len, d):
        pe = torch.zeros(max_len, d)
        pos = torch.arange(max_len).unsqueeze(1).float()
        div = torch.exp(torch.arange(0, d, 2).float() * -(math.log(10000.0)/d))
        pe[:, 0::2] = torch.sin(pos * div)
        pe[:, 1::2] = torch.cos(pos * div)
        return nn.Parameter(pe.unsqueeze(0), requires_grad=False)

    def forward(self, idx):
        B, L = idx.shape
        x = self.tok_emb(idx) + self.pos_emb[:, :L]
        # Causal mask: upper triangular masking
        mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(L, L, dtype=torch.bool, device=idx.device),
                          diagonal=1)
        for block in self.blocks:
            x = block(x, mask)
        x = self.ln_f(x)
        return self.lm_head(x)                      # [B, L, vocab]

# Usage
model = Transformer(vocab_size=10000)
x = torch.randint(0, 10000, (2, 50))   # batch 2, seq 50
logits = model(x)                       # [2, 50, 10000]

These roughly 70 lines implement the core of the Transformer. Real production implementations (LLaMA, Qwen) add a dozen engineering refinements on top: RoPE replacing sinusoidal positional encoding, RMSNorm replacing LayerNorm, SwiGLU replacing the ReLU FFN, GQA replacing MHA, and so on—but the skeleton has not changed in 7 years.

Experimental Results

In the original paper on WMT 2014 English-to-German translation: BLEU 28.4, surpassing Google NMT (LSTM-based) at 27.3 by 1.1 points, while requiring only 1/10 of the training time. That is the key—it wasn't just a marginal improvement in quality; it was fast enough to train much larger models.

Impact

"Attention Is All You Need" is one of the most important papers in the history of AI. Seven years later (2024), only ResNet surpasses it in citation count in the CS field.

Yet its direct contribution was only a 1.1 BLEU improvement in machine translation—what truly changed the world was the possibilities it unlocked:

  1. BERT (2018) and GPT (2018): using the Transformer for pretraining
  2. GPT-3 (2020): proving that the Transformer can scale to tens of billions of parameters and produce emergent capabilities
  3. ViT (2020): bringing the Transformer to computer vision
  4. ChatGPT (2022): Transformer + RLHF as a product
  5. All modern LLMs (2023+): LLaMA, Claude, Qwen, DeepSeek are all Transformer variants

An Interesting Footnote on an Engineering Detail

Section 3.2.2 of the original paper contains the statement: "We found that the Transformer with sinusoidal position encodings performs similarly to learned position embeddings." This claim later proved overly optimistic—learned positional embeddings fail completely when the sequence length exceeds training length, and sinusoidal encodings also extrapolate poorly. This detail was truly resolved by RoPE (P09), five years later.

This is also the value of reading papers: even original authors can be wrong. Understanding these subtleties gives you a clearer picture of how the field evolves.

Why 3 pages instead of 1: If only one paper could be read to understand modern AI, this is it. Mastering the attention mechanism is the entry ticket to all subsequent work. Recommended companion resources: The Annotated Transformer (Harvard NLP) and Andrej Karpathy's "Let's build GPT" video.

P13BERT and GPT: The Twin Stars of the Pretraining Era

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 4, §4.2.5 (Encoder/Decoder divide) · Chapter 3, §3.5.2 (self-supervised pretraining)

BERT: Pre-training of Deep Bidirectional Transformers for Language Understanding
AuthorsJacob Devlin, Ming-Wei Chang, Kenton Lee, Kristina Toutanova (Google)
PublishedNAACL 2019 · arXiv:1810.04805 · 110,000+ citations
Language Models are Few-Shot Learners (GPT-3)
AuthorsTom Brown et al. (OpenAI)
PublishedNeurIPS 2020 · arXiv:2005.14165 · 35,000+ citations

BERT and GPT-3 belong to different technical lineages, but together they defined the "pretraining era." Comparing them side by side is the clearest way to understand both.

BERT: Bidirectional Encoder + MLM

Core innovation: Masked Language Modeling (MLM).

Traditional language models are unidirectional (predicting the next word from left to right). BERT randomly masks 15% of tokens and trains the model to predict the masked content based on bidirectional context. Every position's representation can "see" the entire sentence.

flowchart LR I["The [MASK] sat on the mat"] --> ENC[Transformer Encoder
bidirectional self-attention] ENC --> P["[MASK] = cat (prediction)"]

Second training objective: NSP (Next Sentence Prediction): predicting whether two sentences are consecutive. Subsequent work (RoBERTa 2019) showed that NSP was actually not useful, but MLM has persisted.

The BERT Paradigm

BERT inaugurated the "pretrain + fine-tune" pattern:

  1. Perform MLM pretraining on massive unlabeled text (Wikipedia + BookCorpus, ~3.3B tokens)
  2. Fine-tune for downstream tasks: classification, QA, NER, etc.—only an output head needs to be added

This paradigm reset the state of the art across virtually all NLP tasks in one sweep. BERT-Base (110M parameters), BERT-Large (340M)—large models by the standards of the time, small by today's.

GPT-3: Unidirectional Decoder + Pure Few-Shot

GPT-3 took the opposite approach:

  • - Only uses a Decoder (causal language model, predicting the next token left to right)
  • - No fine-tuning—task instructions and a few examples are written into the prompt, and the model learns "in context"
  • - Dramatic scale increase: 175B parameters, 500× the size of BERT-Large

GPT-3's Key Discovery: In-Context Learning

The true contribution of the GPT-3 paper is not architectural innovation—the architecture is nearly identical to GPT-2. The contribution is demonstrating that: when a model scales to a sufficient size, the ability to "learn a new task from a few examples" emerges.

Example:

Translate English to French:
sea otter => loutre de mer
plush giraffe => girafe peluche
cheese => ???

After seeing just 2 examples, GPT-3 correctly outputs "fromage"—and can generalize to math, reasoning, and coding tasks. This is the starting point at which LLMs transformed from "language models" into "general-purpose reasoning engines."

Comparison of the Two Lineages

BERT (Encoder)GPT (Decoder)
Attention directionBidirectional attentionUnidirectional causal
Training objectiveMLM + NSPNext-token prediction
Task adaptationPretrain + fine-tunePrompting / in-context learning
Typical tasksClassification, NER, QA, sentence embeddingsText generation, dialogue, code
Representative scaleBERT-Large 340MGPT-3 175B
Post-2024 descendantsRoBERTa, DeBERTa, still used for embeddingsLLaMA, Claude, Qwen, DeepSeek — mainstream

Why Decoder-Only Won in the End

Main text §4.2.5 analyzes the key factors:

  1. Decoder-only training is simpler—a single objective, and all text is training data
  2. In-context learning makes "one model for all tasks" a reality
  3. Scaling properties are more favorable for decoder-only—this became consensus after GPT-3
  4. Generation capability naturally encompasses understanding (to write a good answer, you must first understand the question)

Today, BERT-family encoder models are primarily used as "text encoders"—embedding models (BGE, E5), rerankers, and so on. The retrieval stage in most RAG systems still uses encoder models.

Impact

BERT inaugurated the pretraining paradigm—all LLMs today inherit this idea. GPT-3 demonstrated the power of scaling—directly leading to ChatGPT and GPT-4. These two papers are the twin stars of LLM history.

Why You Must Read This: BERT helps you understand the "pretrain-then-fine-tune" paradigm. GPT-3 helps you understand "emergent capabilities" and "in-context learning"—two concepts that are the core assumptions underlying the entire LLM era that followed.

P14Scaling Laws and Chinchilla: The Economics of Training Large Models

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 4, §4.1.1 · Chapter 3, §3.5.2

Scaling Laws for Neural Language Models
AuthorsJared Kaplan et al. (OpenAI)
PublishedarXiv:2001.08361 (2020)
Training Compute-Optimal Large Language Models (Chinchilla)
AuthorsJordan Hoffmann et al. (DeepMind)
PublishedNeurIPS 2022 · arXiv:2203.15556

The Question: Given a Fixed Compute Budget, How Should It Be Split Between Parameters and Data?

Training a large model incurs a total compute cost of roughly C \approx 6 \cdot N \cdot D (N = number of parameters, D = number of training tokens). Suppose you have a fixed C (say, 1e25 FLOPs): is it better to train a large model on a small dataset, or a smaller model on a large dataset?

Kaplan (2020): Loss Follows a Power Law

OpenAI ran hundreds of models of varying sizes and found that loss decreases as a power law with both parameters and data:

L(N, D) \approx E + \frac{A}{N^\alpha} + \frac{B}{D^\beta}

E is the irreducible entropy, \alpha \approx 0.07, \beta \approx 0.28.

Kaplan's key conclusion (later proven incorrect): under a fixed compute budget, the optimal allocation grows the parameter count N much faster than the data D. This inspired the design of GPT-3 at 175B—trained on only 300B tokens (near-optimal by Kaplan's prediction).

Chinchilla (2022): Kaplan Was Wrong

DeepMind redid the experiments with a wider range of N, D combinations. New fitted formula:

L(N, D) = E + \frac{A}{N^{0.34}} + \frac{B}{D^{0.28}}

Key: \alpha changed from 0.07 to 0.34—this means parameters and data should scale in roughly equal proportion.

Specific conclusion: the optimal tokens-to-parameters ratio is approximately 20:1.

ModelParametersTraining tokensActual ratioChinchilla-optimal?
GPT-3175B300B1.7:1❌ Severely data-starved
Chinchilla70B1.4T20:1✅ Optimal
LLaMA-1 65B65B1.4T21.5:1✅ Near-optimal
LLaMA-3 70B70B15T214:1Inference-optimized, far exceeds Chinchilla

Experimental Proof of Chinchilla

Chinchilla (70B parameters, 1.4T training tokens) vs. Gopher (280B parameters, 300B tokens)—under equivalent compute, Chinchilla comprehensively outperformed the 4× larger Gopher on benchmarks like MMLU. "Smaller but better-trained" beats "larger but data-starved"—a significant correction in Scaling Law research.

Training-Optimal vs. Inference-Optimal

Chinchilla optimizes for "lowest training loss." But in practice, models must be run for inference millions of times, so inference efficiency is also an optimization target.

The LLaMA-2/3 series recognized this: deliberately training beyond Chinchilla-optimal—LLaMA-3-70B was trained on 15T tokens (214:1), far past "optimal" in training terms, but yielding a model that is "smaller in parameters yet stronger in capability," making inference cheaper. This is known as "over-training for inference."

Impact

These two papers fundamentally changed decision-making in LLM training:

  1. The "just pile on parameters" approach after GPT-3 was corrected
  2. Data collection and cleaning became equally important as model architecture
  3. The LLaMA series' training data volumes (1.4T → 2T → 15T) stem directly from these insights
Why You Must Read This: If you want to understand the "parameters vs. data" discussion that accompanies every major model release since 2022, these two papers are required reading. They are also the starting point for understanding "why data is so expensive now"—Chinchilla's conclusions set off a data arms race among all major AI companies.

P15RoPE: Rotary Position Embedding

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 4, §4.3.2 Deep Dive on RoPE

RoFormer: Enhanced Transformer with Rotary Position Embedding
AuthorsJianlin Su, Yu Lu, Shengfeng Pan, Ahmed Murtadha, Bo Wen, Yunfeng Liu
AffiliationZhuiyi Technology
PublishedNeurocomputing 2024 · arXiv:2104.09864 (2021)
Citations4,500+ (rapidly growing)

The Problem: Absolute Positional Encodings Cannot Extrapolate

The original Transformer used sinusoidal absolute positional encodings; GPT-2 used learned absolute positional encodings. Both share the same problem—if positions 0–2047 were seen during training, the model fails for positions 2048+. Learned encodings are worse (they break entirely out of range); sinusoidal encodings are slightly better but still don't work. Relative positional encodings (T5 bias, ALiBi) improved matters but had limited expressiveness.

Core Idea: Rotation Instead of Addition

RoPE does not add a positional vector to the input; instead, it rotates Q and K. The key insight: when two vectors are each rotated independently and then dot-producted, the result depends only on their relative angle.

\mathbf{q}_m^\top \mathbf{k}_n = (R_m \mathbf{q})^\top (R_n \mathbf{k}) = \mathbf{q}^\top R_m^\top R_n \mathbf{k} = \mathbf{q}^\top R_{n-m} \mathbf{k}

(Because rotation matrices satisfy R_m^\top R_n = R_{n-m})

So the attention score naturally depends only on the relative position n - m. This is exactly the property that relative positional encodings aim for, achieved here in a more elegant way.

2D Rotation Matrix

A 2D rotation is applied to each pair of adjacent dimensions:

R_m^{(i)} = \begin{pmatrix} \cos m\theta_i & -\sin m\theta_i \\ \sin m\theta_i & \cos m\theta_i \end{pmatrix}, \quad \theta_i = 10000^{-2(i-1)/d}

Different dimensions use different frequencies \theta_i: low dimensions use high frequencies, high dimensions use low frequencies—each dimension captures positional differences at a different scale.

Complex Number Perspective (More Elegant)

Treating a 2D vector as a complex number q = q_1 + iq_2, rotation by m\theta is equivalent to multiplying by e^{im\theta}:

q_m = q \cdot e^{im\theta}, \quad k_n = k \cdot e^{in\theta}
\langle q_m, k_n \rangle = \text{Re}(q \cdot e^{im\theta} \cdot \overline{k \cdot e^{in\theta}}) = \text{Re}(q \bar{k} \cdot e^{i(m-n)\theta})

This depends only on m - n. This is the most mathematically elegant expression of RoPE.

Implementation

def build_rope_cache(d_head, max_len, base=10000, device='cuda'):
    theta = 1.0 / (base ** (torch.arange(0, d_head, 2).float() / d_head))
    t = torch.arange(max_len, device=device).float()
    freqs = torch.outer(t, theta.to(device))            # [max_len, d_head/2]
    return torch.polar(torch.ones_like(freqs), freqs)   # complex form

def apply_rope(x, freqs_cis):
    # x: [B, H, L, d_head]
    x_complex = torch.view_as_complex(
        x.float().reshape(*x.shape[:-1], -1, 2)
    )
    x_rotated = x_complex * freqs_cis[:x.shape[-2]]
    return torch.view_as_real(x_rotated).flatten(-2).type_as(x)

# Usage: apply to Q and K inside attention (not V)
Q = apply_rope(Q, freqs_cis)
K = apply_rope(K, freqs_cis)
# V does not get RoPE — it carries content, not position

Derivative Techniques Built on RoPE

RoPE's continuous trigonometric nature gives it "extrapolation potential"—the following techniques extend RoPE-trained models far beyond their training length:

  • PI (Position Interpolation, Meta 2023): compress positions into the training range, enabling 2–4× extrapolation
  • NTK-aware: compress only high-frequency dimensions while preserving low-frequency ones—enables extrapolation without any fine-tuning
  • YaRN (2023): per-frequency-band refinement + attention temperature adjustment; 1B tokens of fine-tuning enables 32× extrapolation
  • LongRoPE (2024): independently searches for the optimal scaling factor per dimension; feasible up to 2M token contexts

Impact

RoPE is today the de facto standard for open-source LLMs: LLaMA, Qwen, DeepSeek, Mistral, and ChatGLM all use RoPE. The reasons:

  1. Relative position property emerges naturally from the math
  2. Zero additional parameters
  3. Extrapolation-friendly (in combination with YaRN, etc.)
  4. Simple implementation (<30 lines of code)

Interestingly, RoPE's author Jianlin Su (Zhuiyi Technology) was not a researcher at a major lab; the paper was originally published on his personal blog. This is a microcosm of the open-source community's influence—good ideas don't have to come from big companies.

Why You Must Read This: Long context (128K, 1M tokens) is one of the main battlegrounds for LLMs in 2024–2026, and RoPE + YaRN is the underlying technology for that direction. Understanding RoPE is what lets you understand why today's models can support such long contexts.
Ch 4 Related · Efficiency & Inference

P16FlashAttention: IO-Aware Exact Attention

Main text reference: Chapter 3, §3.6.3 Operator Optimization · Chapter 4, §4.2.2 / §4.8

FlashAttention: Fast and Memory-Efficient Exact Attention with IO-Awareness
AuthorsTri Dao, Daniel Y. Fu, Stefano Ermon, Atri Rudra, Christopher Ré
AffiliationStanford University
PublishedNeurIPS 2022 · arXiv:2205.14135 · 5,000+ citations
Follow-upFlashAttention-2 (2023), FlashAttention-3 (2024, FP8 + H100 optimizations)

The Problem: Attention's Real Bottleneck Is Not FLOPs

Self-attention has time complexity O(L^2 d) for long sequences, often labeled the "compute bottleneck." But Tri Dao observed that on GPUs, attention is actually a memory-bandwidth bottleneck, not a compute bottleneck.

The numbers: an A100 GPU delivers 312 TFLOPs for FP16 GEMM, but its HBM bandwidth is only 1.5 TB/s. The ratio (arithmetic intensity) needs to reach ~208 FLOP/byte to saturate compute — and standard attention falls far below this. Most of the time, the GPU is stalled waiting for data to move from HBM.

The Problem with Standard Attention

A standard PyTorch implementation does four things:

  1. Compute S = QK^\top \in \mathbb{R}^{L \times L}, write it to HBM
  2. Read S from HBM, compute P = \text{softmax}(S), write it back to HBM
  3. Read P from HBM, multiply by V to get O, write it back to HBM
  4. (Possible dropout and masking operations add further read/write passes)

At L = 4096, the S matrix alone is 64 MB. Each read/write is a 128 MB round-trip to HBM. As L grows, this cost scales linearly while compute utilization stays extremely low.

Core Method: Tiling + Online Softmax

FlashAttention splits Q, K, V into tiles sized to fit in the GPU's on-chip SRAM. The outer loop iterates over Q tiles Q_i; the inner loop iterates over K, V tiles (K_j, V_j). The full S or P matrix is never materialized — all intermediate results stay in SRAM.

flowchart TB HBM["HBM — large, slow"] -. "read Q_i, K_j, V_j" .-> SRAM SRAM["SRAM — small, fast
tile-level computation"] -. "write O_i" .-> HBM SRAM --> S["S_ij = Q_i K_j^T (in SRAM)"] S --> SM["online softmax: update m, l, O"] SM --> NEXT["next K_j, V_j tile"]

The Key Challenge: Online Softmax

Softmax requires normalization — \text{softmax}(x_i) = \exp(x_i) / \sum_j \exp(x_j). It seems like you must see all x_j before computing the denominator. FlashAttention uses an incremental update instead: each time a new S_{ij} tile arrives, it maintains a running max and running sum, producing a result that is mathematically equivalent to the full softmax.

Say we have processed up to tile j-1, with state (m_{j-1}, l_{j-1}, O_{j-1}) — the current running max m, running denominator l, and current weighted output O. A new tile (K_j, V_j) arrives:

Compute S_{ij} = Q_i K_j^\top. Let \tilde{m}_j = \max(S_{ij}). New max:

m_j = \max(m_{j-1}, \tilde{m}_j)

Rescale both the new and old exponential terms using the new max:

l_j = e^{m_{j-1} - m_j} \cdot l_{j-1} + \sum_k e^{S_{ij,k} - m_j}

New output (old contribution is rescaled to the new max):

O_j = \frac{1}{l_j}\left[ l_{j-1} e^{m_{j-1} - m_j} O_{j-1} + \sum_k e^{S_{ij,k} - m_j} V_{j,k} \right]

After scanning all K/V tiles, O_J is exactly equivalent to the full softmax result. It is numerically identical to standard attention — this is not an approximation.

Pseudocode

def flash_attention(Q, K, V, block_size=64):
    """Q, K, V: [L, d].  Returns O: [L, d], never materializing the LxL matrix S"""
    L, d = Q.shape
    O = torch.zeros_like(Q)
    m = torch.full((L, 1), -float('inf'))  # running max per row
    l = torch.zeros((L, 1))                 # running sum per row

    for j in range(0, L, block_size):
        K_j = K[j:j+block_size]              # load tile into SRAM
        V_j = V[j:j+block_size]
        S_ij = Q @ K_j.T / math.sqrt(d)      # computed in SRAM, [L, block]

        # Causal mask (if needed)
        mask = torch.arange(j, j+K_j.shape[0]).unsqueeze(0) > \
               torch.arange(L).unsqueeze(1)
        S_ij = S_ij.masked_fill(mask, -float('inf'))

        m_new = torch.maximum(m, S_ij.max(dim=-1, keepdim=True).values)
        P_ij = torch.exp(S_ij - m_new)
        l_new = torch.exp(m - m_new) * l + P_ij.sum(dim=-1, keepdim=True)

        # Rescale old O to new max, add current tile's contribution
        O = (l / l_new) * torch.exp(m - m_new) * O + (P_ij @ V_j) / l_new
        m, l = m_new, l_new

    return O

The actual CUDA implementation involves substantial engineering — warp-level shuffling, register reuse, pipelining — but the core algorithm is exactly what is shown above.

Performance Gains

  • - HBM accesses: reduced from O(L^2 d) to O(L^2 d^2 / M) (M = SRAM size) — several orders of magnitude fewer
  • - Speed: 2–4× faster than standard attention on A100, with greater gains at longer sequences
  • - Memory: intermediate S and P are never materialized, reducing peak memory from O(L^2) to O(L) — this is what makes 128K+ context windows feasible

FlashAttention-2 and -3

FlashAttention-2 (2023): Refined outer loop ordering, reduced non-matmul operations, improved warp partitioning. Another 2× speedup, totaling 4–8× over standard attention.

FlashAttention-3 (2024): Targets the H100 Hopper architecture, leveraging FP8 Tensor Cores, asynchronous execution, and more granular pipelining. Achieves ~75% of peak throughput on H100.

These three generations represent the most important attention optimization work from 2022 to 2024, and directly influenced the implementations of PyTorch SDPA, vLLM, xFormers, and TensorRT-LLM. Modern LLM training and inference almost universally defaults to FlashAttention.

Key Insight

FlashAttention's impact extends well beyond attention itself — it is the exemplar of IO-aware algorithm design. Many GPU kernels can be restructured similarly: keep intermediate results in SRAM, and use mathematically equivalent incremental forms to avoid large intermediate buffers. FlashDecoding and RingAttention are direct successors of this idea.

Why You Must Read This: The online softmax algorithm is something you need to work through by hand to truly understand. Once you grasp it, you will recognize the same technique appearing in many other places — PagedAttention, StreamingLLM, and FlashDecoding all rely on the same mathematical structure.

P17LoRA / QLoRA: Making Fine-Tuning Affordable

Main text reference: Chapter 3, §3.5.3 Fine-Tuning · Chapter 4, §4.4.1 Low-Rank Ideas

LoRA: Low-Rank Adaptation of Large Language Models
AuthorsEdward Hu, Yelong Shen, Phillip Wallis, Zeyuan Allen-Zhu, Yuanzhi Li, Shean Wang, Lu Wang, Weizhu Chen
AffiliationMicrosoft
PublishedICLR 2022 · arXiv:2106.09685 · 8,000+ citations
QLoRA: Efficient Finetuning of Quantized LLMs
AuthorsTim Dettmers et al. (University of Washington)
PublishedNeurIPS 2023 · arXiv:2305.14314

The Problem: Full Fine-Tuning a Large Model Is Too Expensive

Fine-tuning a 70B model requires storing: parameters at 140 GB (FP16) + gradients at 140 GB + Adam optimizer state at 560 GB ≈ 840 GB total, plus activations. Even a multi-GPU single node isn't enough — you need ZeRO-3-level distributed training.

Yet many use cases only need light customization — adding a domain-specific style, or making the model follow a particular output format. Is it really necessary to update every parameter?

LoRA's Core Hypothesis

Hu et al. hypothesize that when a model adapts to a downstream task, the weight update \Delta W has a low-rank structure.

That is, \Delta W \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times k} can be decomposed as \Delta W = BA, where B \in \mathbb{R}^{d \times r}, A \in \mathbb{R}^{r \times k}, and r \ll \min(d, k).

During training, the original weights W_0 are frozen; only A and B are trained:

W = W_0 + \Delta W = W_0 + BA, \quad h = W_0 x + BA x
flowchart LR X[x] --> W0["W_0 (frozen, d x k)"] W0 --> Y1[W_0 x] X --> A["A (r x k, trainable)"] A --> B["B (d x r, trainable)"] B --> Y2["BA x"] Y1 --> ADD((+)) Y2 --> ADD ADD --> H[h]

Initialization: A is initialized with Gaussian noise; B is initialized to zero — so the starting point BA = 0, matching full fine-tuning's initial state.

Parameter Count Comparison

For GPT-3 175B, consider a layer where W \in \mathbb{R}^{12288 \times 12288}, with 150M parameters. With r = 8: LoRA introduces A \in \mathbb{R}^{8 \times 12288} and B \in \mathbb{R}^{12288 \times 8}, for a total of 200K parameters — a 750× reduction.

Across the full model, LoRA trains approximately 0.01% of parameters. Memory requirements are dramatically reduced: frozen weights require no Adam state or gradient storage — only A and B need gradients — yielding 10–20× memory savings over full fine-tuning.

Implementation (PEFT)

from peft import LoraConfig, get_peft_model
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM

base = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained("meta-llama/Llama-3-8B")

config = LoraConfig(
    r=16,                          # rank
    lora_alpha=32,                 # scaling factor; effective W += (alpha/r) BA
    target_modules=["q_proj", "v_proj"],  # apply LoRA only to Q and V projections
    lora_dropout=0.05,
    task_type="CAUSAL_LM",
)
model = get_peft_model(base, config)
model.print_trainable_parameters()
# trainable params: 4,194,304 || all params: 8,034,455,552 || trainable%: 0.05%

Merging at Inference Time

At inference, BA can be merged back into W_0: W_{\text{new}} = W_0 + BA, producing a standard weight matrix. Zero additional inference overhead — this is a major advantage over Adapters, which insert small modules between layers and increase latency at inference time.

QLoRA: Another 4× Memory Reduction

QLoRA (2023) quantizes the base model to 4-bit while keeping the LoRA parameters in FP16. Memory breakdown:

  • - Frozen weights: 140 GB (FP16) → 35 GB (INT4), 4× saving
  • - LoRA parameters and gradients: tens of MB, negligible
  • - Total: a 65B model fits on a single 48 GB GPU

QLoRA introduces several technical innovations:

  1. NF4 (NormalFloat 4-bit): a 4-bit data type designed for normally distributed weights, achieving higher precision than standard INT4
  2. Double quantization: quantizes the quantization scales themselves, further reducing memory
  3. Paged optimizers: uses NVIDIA Unified Memory to swap optimizer state to CPU when GPU memory is insufficient

QLoRA made "fine-tuning a 65B model on a consumer GPU" a reality — it was the inflection point that opened large-scale fine-tuning to the open-source community. Projects like Alpaca and Vicuna all relied on LoRA/QLoRA.

Variants

  • LoRA+: uses different learning rates for A and B (B should be larger), a small change that yields 1–2% quality improvement
  • DoRA (Weight-Decomposed LoRA): decomposes weights into magnitude and direction, adapting each separately
  • AdaLoRA: dynamically allocates rank per layer
  • rsLoRA: improved scaling rules to stabilize training at larger ranks

Impact

LoRA is the most important fine-tuning work since 2022. Nearly every open-source LLM fine-tune today uses LoRA or one of its variants — HuggingFace's PEFT library was built around it. LoRA also inspired architectural thinking: DeepSeek's MLA uses low-rank decomposition to compress the KV Cache (main text §4.4.1), a direct conceptual descendant.

Why You Must Read This: LoRA is the most important "lever" in the open-source community — it lets individuals and small teams participate in frontier LLM adaptation. QLoRA's NF4 design is worth reading carefully; it offers excellent intuition about data type design tailored to a distribution.

P18PagedAttention / vLLM: Treating KV Cache Like Virtual Memory

Main text reference: Chapter 4, §4.8.3

Efficient Memory Management for Large Language Model Serving with PagedAttention
AuthorsWoosuk Kwon, Zhuohan Li, Siyuan Zhuang, Ying Sheng, Lianmin Zheng, Cody Hao Yu, Joseph E. Gonzalez, Hao Zhang, Ion Stoica
AffiliationUC Berkeley, Stanford, UCSD, CMU
PublishedSOSP 2023 · arXiv:2309.06180

The Problem: Memory Fragmentation in KV Cache

LLM inference requires contiguous KV Cache space per request. A naive implementation pre-allocates by max_seq_len: a request reserving space for 4096 tokens might only use 500, wasting 87%. There are four categories of waste at the system level:

  1. Reservation waste: space reserved for sequences that might grow longer
  2. Internal fragmentation: each request has a different actual length, so reserved space goes unused
  3. External fragmentation: small gaps left by completed short requests can't fit new larger ones
  4. No sharing: requests with identical prefixes each store their own copy (pure waste)

Core Method: Bringing OS Virtual Memory to KV Cache

PagedAttention directly mirrors OS virtual memory:

  • GPU memory is divided into fixed-size blocks (KV data for 16 tokens per block)
  • Each request maintains a block table mapping logical token positions to physical blocks
  • Blocks are allocated on demand and grow dynamically with token generation — no pre-reservation
  • The attention kernel accesses KV data indirectly via the block table
flowchart TB subgraph GPU["GPU Memory Pool (blocks of 16 tokens KV)"] B0[Block 0: free] B3[Block 3: R1 tokens 16-19] B5[Block 5: R2 tokens 0-14] B7[Block 7: R1 tokens 0-15] B8[Block 8: free] end R1["Request 1 (20 tokens)
block_table: [7, 3]"] --> B7 R1 --> B3 R2["Request 2 (15 tokens)
block_table: [5]"] --> B5

Modified Attention Kernel

The standard kernel assumes K and V are contiguous. PagedAttention's kernel uses the block table for indirect lookup:

// Simplified pseudocode
for each query q_i:
    output = 0
    max_score = -inf; sum_exp = 0
    for each logical token j in query's block_table:
        block_id  = block_table[j / BLOCK_SIZE]
        inner_idx = j % BLOCK_SIZE
        k_j = K_pool[block_id * BLOCK_SIZE + inner_idx]
        v_j = V_pool[block_id * BLOCK_SIZE + inner_idx]
        score = q_i @ k_j / sqrt(d)
        // online softmax update (same as FlashAttention)
        ...

The one extra indirection (block_table[...]) adds a small overhead, but the memory savings far outweigh this cost.

Sharing and Copy-on-Write

When multiple requests share the same prefix (e.g., a system prompt or few-shot examples), their block tables can point to the same physical blocks. A copy is made only when divergence occurs — when a request starts generating different tokens. This makes prefix caching a natural optimization, directly benefiting high-concurrency LLM serving.

Beam search and parallel sampling benefit similarly: multiple candidates share the prefix KV Cache.

Measured Gains

Paper results (LLaMA-13B):

  • - Memory utilization: ~40% → ~96%
  • - Concurrent requests per GPU: 4–10×
  • - Throughput: 2–4× improvement

vLLM

The paper authors simultaneously open-sourced vLLM — a production-grade inference engine combining PagedAttention, continuous batching, and speculative decoding. Since its mid-2023 release:

  • - It became the de facto standard for open-source LLM serving
  • - In 2024, it ranked alongside SGLang as one of the top two inference frameworks by OpenRank
  • - A major v1 architectural overhaul shipped in 2025
  • - Adopted by virtually every major cloud provider and LLM service

vLLM's success exceeds the citation value of the PagedAttention paper itself — it is a textbook example of "research result becoming industrial infrastructure."

Key Insight

This is a classic example of cross-domain borrowing. The virtual memory concept was mature by 1961 (the Atlas computer) — over 60 years later, it was transplanted into LLM inference to solve a new problem. A programmer who studied operating systems can read this paper and understand it almost instantly. That is the power of foundational knowledge.

Why You Must Read This: Without this paper, you won't understand why vLLM can be 5–10× faster than a naive implementation. For systems engineers, it is also an excellent model of cross-domain knowledge transfer.

P19Switch Transformer & Mixtral: Making MoE Practical

Main text reference: Chapter 4, §4.6

Switch Transformers: Scaling to Trillion Parameter Models with Simple and Efficient Sparsity
AuthorsWilliam Fedus, Barret Zoph, Noam Shazeer (Google)
PublishedJMLR 2022 · arXiv:2101.03961
Mixtral of Experts
AuthorsMistral AI team
PublishedarXiv:2401.04088 (2024)

MoE Is an Old Idea

The MoE concept traces back to Jacobs et al.'s 1991 "Adaptive Mixtures of Local Experts." Shazeer et al. first applied it to deep learning in 2017 (Sparsely-Gated MoE). But it was Switch Transformer and Mixtral that truly demonstrated MoE's practical value.

Switch Transformer: Top-1 Routing

Switch's core simplification: each token is routed to exactly 1 expert (prior work typically used top-2). This drastically simplifies the gating mechanism and reduces communication complexity.

\text{expert}(x) = \arg\max_i g_i(x), \quad g(x) = \text{softmax}(W_r x)

If expert i is selected, the output is:

y = g_i(x) \cdot \text{Expert}_i(x)

The key engineering challenge is load balancing. If routing is uneven — some experts overloaded, others idle — GPU utilization suffers. Switch adds an auxiliary loss:

\mathcal{L}_{\text{aux}} = \alpha \cdot N \sum_{i=1}^N f_i \cdot P_i

Here f_i is the fraction of tokens routed to expert i, and P_i is the average gate probability for expert i across all tokens. At the optimum, f_i = P_i = 1/N and \mathcal{L}_{\text{aux}} = 1.

Switch Transformer trained a 1.6 trillion parameter model — the first publicly disclosed "trillion-parameter LLM." It shocked the field at the time. However, its quality advantage over dense models of the same compute budget was modest, and the community remained skeptical about whether MoE was truly worthwhile.

Mixtral 8x7B: MoE Proves Itself

Mistral AI's late-2023 release of Mixtral 8x7B changed the status of MoE entirely:

  • - 8 experts, each roughly the size of a 7B parameter model
  • - Top-2 routing (rather than Switch's Top-1)
  • - Total parameters: 47B (with shared attention); activated parameters per token: ~13B
  • - Quality comparable to LLaMA-2 70B (dense), at the inference speed of a 13B model
flowchart TB X[Token x] --> R[Router: softmax W_r x] R -->|top-2 gate| G["g_i=0.6
g_j=0.4"] G --> E1[Expert 1 FFN] G --> E2[Expert 2 FFN] E1 -->|w=0.6| SUM((+)) E2 -->|w=0.4| SUM SUM --> Y[Output] E3[Expert 3 FFN] -.-> SUM E4[Expert 4 FFN] -.-> SUM E5[...] -.-> SUM

Mixtral matched LLaMA-2 70B on MMLU, math, and coding benchmarks, while being significantly faster to run. This was the first clear demonstration that "MoE achieves the same capability at lower cost."

Engineering Challenges of MoE

MoE looks appealing on paper but is difficult in practice. Key engineering challenges:

  1. Memory footprint: all experts must be loaded at inference time (you don't know routing in advance). Mixtral 8x7B requires 80 GB+ of GPU memory — it is not as lean as the "13B activated" framing suggests
  2. Load imbalance: even with auxiliary loss, some experts still get overloaded. A "capacity factor" of 1.25 (allowing 25% overflow) reduces token dropping
  3. Small-batch inefficiency: with small batches, each expert receives very few tokens, and GPU utilization is low. MoE works best at large batch sizes
  4. All-to-all communication: in distributed MoE, tokens must be routed to different devices per layer — two all-to-all operations per layer, with significant communication overhead

Subsequent Evolution

DeepSeek-V2/V3 (2024) pushed MoE further:

  • Fine-grained experts: moved from 8 large experts to 256 small experts, enabling far more diverse combinations
  • Shared experts: 1–2 experts that every token passes through (handling general knowledge), with other experts gated
  • Multi-token prediction, MLA attention compression, and FP8 training combined with MoE to further improve efficiency

Qwen3-Next, Kimi K2, GLM-4.5, and LLaMA 4 all follow the fine-grained MoE path. By late 2025, nearly every frontier open-source model is a MoE.

Why You Must Read This: MoE is the core technology enabling large-scale model scaling. Reading both Switch and Mixtral gives you a full picture of "sparse activation" as an engineering principle — one idea (compute only part of the parameters) reshaped the entire open-source LLM landscape from 2024 to 2026.
PART FOUR

Chapter 4 · Alignment & Applications

RLHF, DPO, CoT, DeepSeek-V3 — 4 key papers that took LLMs from "can talk" to "can do things."

P20Mamba: Linear-Time Sequence Modeling

Corresponds to main text: Chapter 4, Section 4.4.3 — Non-Transformer Architectures

Mamba: Linear-Time Sequence Modeling with Selective State Spaces
AuthorsAlbert Gu, Tri Dao
AffiliationCarnegie Mellon University, Princeton University
PublishedCOLM 2024 (preprint 2023)
Citations3,000+
LinkarXiv:2312.00752

The Problem: Transformer's Quadratic Complexity

Transformer's self-attention is O(N^2) in both time and memory with respect to sequence length N — a fundamental limitation. FlashAttention (P10) reduces the constant factor through hardware-level optimization, but the quadratic asymptotic complexity remains. For ultra-long sequences at the million-token scale (genomes, long videos, code repositories), Transformers remain impractical.

From 2020 to 2023, Gu explored State Space Models (SSMs) — a sequence modeling framework rooted in control theory with O(N) complexity. Prior work S4 (2021) and H3 (2022) demonstrated feasibility, but these models consistently fell short of Transformers in language modeling. Mamba is the breakthrough in this line of research.

Background: Structured State Space Models (S4)

The continuous-time SSM is defined as:

h'(t) = Ah(t) + Bx(t)
y(t) = Ch(t) + Dx(t)

After discretization (using zero-order hold or bilinear transform):

h_t = \bar{A} h_{t-1} + \bar{B} x_t
y_t = C h_t + D x_t

This is a linear recurrence — similar to an RNN, but with fixed parameters \bar{A}, \bar{B}, C that do not depend on the input. Fixed parameters mean: the recurrence can be unrolled into a convolution and accelerated with FFT (O(N \log N) training), while also supporting O(1) per-step inference via the recurrence form.

S4's limitation: because A, B, C do not depend on the input, the model cannot selectively remember or forget information based on content. This is precisely the core strength of Transformer attention (attention weights are entirely determined by the input).

Core Innovation: Selective State Spaces

Mamba's key insight: make B, C, and the discretization step size \Delta input-dependent:

B_t = \text{Linear}(x_t), \quad C_t = \text{Linear}(x_t), \quad \Delta_t = \text{softplus}(\text{Linear}(x_t))

This allows the model to decide "how much to remember" and "what to output" based on the content of the current token. \Delta_t controls the discretization step size — when \Delta is large, \bar{A} approaches 0 (forgetting history); when \Delta is small, \bar{A} approaches 1 (retaining history).

Input dependence means FFT can no longer be used (the convolution kernel is no longer fixed). Gu and Dao (also the author of FlashAttention) designed a hardware-aware selective scan algorithm — essentially replacing the naive recurrence with parallel prefix sums (parallel scan) for efficient GPU execution.

flowchart TB X["Input x_t"] --> Lin1["Linear -> B_t"] X --> Lin2["Linear -> C_t"] X --> Lin3["Linear -> Delta_t"] Lin3 --> Disc["Discretize A, B"] Lin1 --> Disc Disc --> Scan["Selective scan (parallel scan)"] H["h_{t-1}"] --> Scan Scan --> H2["h_t"] H2 --> Out["y_t = C_t * h_t"] Lin2 --> Out

Mamba Block Architecture

The full Mamba block is more than just the SSM — it also wraps a structure similar to the Transformer FFN:

class MambaBlock(nn.Module):
    """Simplified Mamba block"""
    def __init__(self, d_model, d_state=16, d_conv=4, expand=2):
        super().__init__()
        d_inner = d_model * expand
        self.in_proj = nn.Linear(d_model, d_inner * 2, bias=False)  # split into two branches
        self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(d_inner, d_inner, d_conv, padding=d_conv-1,
                                groups=d_inner)  # local convolution
        self.act = nn.SiLU()

        # SSM parameters (input-dependent)
        self.x_proj = nn.Linear(d_inner, d_state * 2 + 1, bias=False)  # B, C, delta
        self.A_log = nn.Parameter(torch.log(torch.arange(1, d_state+1).float()))
        self.D = nn.Parameter(torch.ones(d_inner))

        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(d_inner, d_model, bias=False)

    def forward(self, x):
        # x: (batch, seq_len, d_model)
        xz = self.in_proj(x)
        x_branch, z = xz.chunk(2, dim=-1)

        # Local convolution (captures nearby context)
        x_branch = self.conv1d(x_branch.transpose(1, 2))[:, :, :x.size(1)]
        x_branch = x_branch.transpose(1, 2)
        x_branch = self.act(x_branch)

        # Selective SSM
        y = self.selective_ssm(x_branch)
        y = y + self.D.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0) * x_branch  # skip connection

        # Gated output
        output = y * self.act(z)
        return self.out_proj(output)

    def selective_ssm(self, x):
        # Compute B, C, delta from input
        proj = self.x_proj(x)
        # ... discretization + parallel scan (in practice uses a Triton kernel)
        pass  # full implementation requires selective_scan_cuda

Complexity Comparison

\lt td>O(N d) (KV cache) \lt td>O(d) \lt td>O(d) (fixed state)
ModelTraining ComplexityInference (per token)Long-Sequence Support
TransformerO(N^2 d) Limited by quadratic complexity
S4 (fixed SSM)O(N \log N \cdot d) Good, but lacks selectivity
MambaO(N d) Theoretically unbounded

Experimental Results

  • - Language modeling: Mamba-3B matches same-parameter-count Transformer++ perplexity on The Pile
  • - Inference throughput: at sequence length 64K, Mamba is 5× faster than Transformer
  • - DNA modeling: substantially surpasses Transformers on the HG38 human genome (natural advantage on ultra-long sequences)
  • - Audio: leads on SC09 speech classification

Impact and Controversy

Mamba sparked a wave of interest in "Transformer alternatives":

  • Mamba-2 (Gu & Dao, 2024): unifies SSMs into a matrix multiplication framework for further speedup
  • Jamba (AI21, 2024): hybrid Mamba + Transformer architecture
